<span class="toctext">Contemporary</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Historical"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Historical</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Genocide_question"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Genocide question</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Memorials"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Memorials</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_and_contributing_factors">Causes and contributing factors</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_Great_Famine" title="Chronology of the Great Famine">Chronology of the Great Famine</a></div>\n<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union</a> in January 1801, Ireland had been part of the United Kingdom. <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">Executive power</a> lay in the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Ireland" title="Lord Lieutenant of Ireland">Lord Lieutenant of Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_Secretary_for_Ireland" title="Chief Secretary for Ireland">Chief Secretary for Ireland</a>, who were appointed by the British government. Ireland sent 105 members of parliament to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons of the United Kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_Ireland" title="Peerage of Ireland">Irish</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_peer" title="Representative peer">representative peers</a> elected 28 of their own number to sit for life in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>. Between 1832 and 1859, 70% of Irish representatives were landowners or the sons of landowners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir1995&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir1995[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 40 years that followed the union, successive British governments grappled with the problems of governing a country which had, as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a> put it in 1844, "a starving population, an absentee aristocracy, an alien <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">established Protestant church</a>, and in addition the weakest executive in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake1967179_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake1967179-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> One historian calculated that, between 1801 and 1845, there had been 114 commissions and 61 special committees enquiring into the state of Ireland, and that "without exception their findings prophesied disaster; Ireland was on the verge of starvation, her population rapidly increasing, three-quarters of her labourers unemployed, housing conditions appalling and the standard of living unbelievably low".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199136_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199136-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lectures printed in 1847 by <a href="/wiki/John_Hughes_(archbishop_of_New_York)" title="John Hughes (archbishop of New York)">John Hughes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_New_York" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of New York">Bishop of New York</a>, are a contemporary exploration into the antecendent causes, particularly the political climate, in which the Irish famine occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_that_restricted_the_rights_of_Irish_Catholics">Laws that restricted the rights of Irish Catholics</span></h3>\n<p>In the 17th and 18th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Catholic">Irish Catholics</a> had been prohibited by the <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws_(Ireland)" title="Penal Laws (Ireland)">penal laws</a> from purchasing or leasing land, from voting, from holding political office, from living in or within 5 miles (8&#160;km) of a corporate town, from obtaining education, from entering a profession, and from doing many other things necessary for a person to succeed and prosper in society. The laws had largely <a href="/wiki/Catholic_emancipation" title="Catholic emancipation">been reformed</a> by 1793, and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" title="Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829">Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829</a> allowed Irish Catholics to again sit in parliament.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landlords_and_tenants">Landlords and tenants</span></h3>\n<p>During the 18th century, the "middleman system" for managing landed property was introduced. Rent collection was left in the hands of the landlords\' agents, or middlemen. This assured the landlord of a regular income, and relieved them of direct responsibility, while leaving tenants open to exploitation by the middlemen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Catholics, the bulk of whom lived in conditions of <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a> and insecurity despite <a href="/wiki/Catholic_emancipation" title="Catholic emancipation">Catholic emancipation</a> in 1829, made up 80% of the population. At the top of the "social pyramid" was the "<a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">ascendancy class</a>", the English and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_people" title="Anglo-Irish people">Anglo-Irish</a> families who owned most of the land, and held more or less unchecked power over their tenants. Some of their estates were vast; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Lucan" title="Earl of Lucan">Earl of Lucan</a> owned over 60,000 acres (240&#160;km<sup>2</sup>). Many of these landlords lived in England and were known as <a href="/wiki/Absentee_landlord" title="Absentee landlord">absentee landlords</a>. The rent revenue\xe2\x80\x94collected from "impoverished tenants" who were paid minimal wages to raise crops and livestock for export<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaxton1997&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaxton1997[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94was mostly sent to England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELitton1994&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELitton1994[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1843, the British Government considered that the land question in Ireland was the root cause of disaffection in the country. They established a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Commission">Royal Commission</a>, chaired by the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Devon" title="Earl of Devon">Earl of Devon</a>, to enquire into the laws regarding the occupation of land. <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a> described this commission as "perfectly one-sided", being composed of landlords, with no tenant representation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199120\xe2\x80\x9321_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199120\xe2\x80\x9321-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In February 1845, Devon reported:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>It would be impossible adequately to describe the privations which they [the Irish labourer and his family] habitually and silently endure&#160;... in many districts their only food is the potato, their only beverage water&#160;... their cabins are seldom a protection against the weather&#160;... a bed or a blanket is a rare luxury&#160;... and nearly in all their pig and a manure heap constitute their only property.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199124_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199124-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The Commissioners concluded they could not "forbear expressing our strong sense of the patient endurance which the labouring classes have exhibited under sufferings greater, we believe, than the people of any other country in Europe have to sustain".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199124_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199124-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The Commission stated that bad relations between landlord and tenant were principally responsible. There was no hereditary loyalty, feudal tie, or mitigating tradition of paternalism as existed in England (Ireland was a conquered country). The <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Clare" title="Earl of Clare">Earl of Clare</a> observed of landlords that "confiscation is their common title".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199121_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199121-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> According to the historian <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Woodham-Smith" title="Cecil Woodham-Smith">Cecil Woodham-Smith</a>, landlords regarded the land as simply a source of income, from which as much as possible was to be extracted. With the Irish "brooding over their discontent in sullen indignation" (in the words of the Earl of Clare), the countryside was largely viewed by landlords as a hostile place in which to live, and absentee ownership was common; some landlords visited their property only once or twice in a lifetime, if ever.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199121_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199121-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The rents from Ireland were generally spent elsewhere; an estimated \xc2\xa36,000,000 was remitted out of Ireland in 1842.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199121_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199121-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ability of middlemen was measured by the rent income they could contrive to extract from tenants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> They were described in evidence before the Commission as "land sharks", "bloodsuckers", and "the most oppressive species of tyrant that ever lent assistance to the destruction of a country".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The middlemen leased large tracts of land from the landlords on long leases with fixed rents, which they then sublet as they saw fit. They would split a holding into smaller and smaller parcels so as to increase the amount of rent they could obtain. Tenants could be evicted for reasons such as non-payment of rents (which were high), or a landlord\'s decision to raise sheep instead of <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grain crops</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Cotter_(farmer)" title="Cotter (farmer)">cottier</a> paid his rent by working for the landlord.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELitton20069\xe2\x80\x9310_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELitton20069\xe2\x80\x9310-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As any improvement made on a holding by a tenant became the property of the landlord when the lease expired or was terminated, the incentive to make improvements was limited. Most tenants had no security of tenure on the land; as tenants "at will", they could be turned out whenever the landlord chose. The only exception to this arrangement was in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> where, under a practice known as <a href="/wiki/Custom_of_Ulster" title="Custom of Ulster">"tenant right"</a>, a tenant was compensated for any improvement they made to their holding. According to Woodham-Smith, the commission stated that "the superior prosperity and tranquility of Ulster, compared with the rest of Ireland, were due to tenant right".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199122-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Landlords in Ireland often used their powers without compunction, and tenants lived in dread of them. Woodham-Smith writes that, in these circumstances, "industry and enterprise were extinguished and a peasantry created which was one of the most destitute in Europe".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199124_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199124-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Tenants.2C_subdivisions.2C_and_bankruptcy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenants,_subdivisions,_and_bankruptcy">Tenants, subdivisions, and bankruptcy</span></h3>\n<p>In 1845, 24% of all Irish tenant farms were of 0.4\xe2\x80\x932&#160;hectares (1\xe2\x80\x935&#160;acres) in size, while 40% were of 2\xe2\x80\x936&#160;hectares (5\xe2\x80\x9315&#160;acres). Holdings were so small that no crop other than potatoes would suffice to feed a family. Shortly before the famine the British government reported that poverty was so widespread that one-third of all Irish small holdings could not support their families after paying their rent, except by earnings of seasonal migrant labour in England and Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKee199315_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKee199315-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Following the famine, reforms were implemented making it illegal to further divide land holdings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrisUris200315_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrisUris200315-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1841 census showed a population of just over eight million. Two-thirds of those depended on agriculture for their survival, but they rarely received a working wage. They had to work for their landlords in return for the patch of land they needed to grow enough food for their own families. This was the system which forced Ireland and its peasantry into <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a>, since only the potato could be grown in sufficient quantity. The rights to a plot of land in Ireland could mean the difference between life and death in the early 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaxton1997&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaxton1997[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2018]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2018)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potato_dependency">Potato dependency</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:An_Irish_Peasant_Family_Discovering_the_Blight_of_their_Store_by_Daniel_MacDonald.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/An_Irish_Peasant_Family_Discovering_the_Blight_of_their_Store_by_Daniel_MacDonald.jpg/300px-An_Irish_Peasant_Family_Discovering_the_Blight_of_their_Store_by_Daniel_MacDonald.jpg" width="300" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/An_Irish_Peasant_Family_Discovering_the_Blight_of_their_Store_by_Daniel_MacDonald.jpg/450px-An_Irish_Peasant_Family_Discovering_the_Blight_of_their_Store_by_Daniel_MacDonald.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/An_Irish_Peasant_Family_Discovering_the_Blight_of_their_Store_by_Daniel_MacDonald.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:An_Irish_Peasant_Family_Discovering_the_Blight_of_their_Store_by_Daniel_MacDonald.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>An Irish Peasant Family Discovering the Blight of their Store</i> by Cork artist Daniel MacDonald, <i>c.</i> 1847. For economic reasons, the Irish peasantry had become dependent on potato crop.</div></div></div>\n<p>The potato was introduced to Ireland as a garden crop of the gentry. By the late 17th century, it had become widespread as a supplementary rather than a principal food because the main diet still revolved around butter, milk, and grain products. However, in the first two decades of the 18th century, it became a base food of the poor, especially in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199519\xe2\x80\x9320_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199519\xe2\x80\x9320-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, a disproportionate share of the potatoes grown in Ireland were of a single variety, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Lumper" title="Irish Lumper">Irish Lumper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IrishCentral_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrishCentral-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The expansion of the economy between 1760 and 1815 saw the potato make inroads into the diet of the people and become a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> year round for farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199520_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199520-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The large dependency on this single crop, and the lack of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variability" title="Genetic variability">genetic variability</a> among the potato plants in Ireland (a <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a>), were two of the reasons why the emergence of <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">Phytophthora infestans</a></i> had such devastating effects in Ireland and less severe <a href="/wiki/European_Potato_Failure" title="European Potato Failure">effects elsewhere in Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Potatoes were essential to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Cottier_(farmer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cottier (farmer)">cottier system</a>, supporting an extremely cheap workforce, but at the cost of lower living standards. For the labourer, it was essentially a potato wage that shaped the expanding agrarian economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199520_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199520-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The expansion of tillage led to an inevitable expansion of the potato acreage and an expansion of peasant farmers. By 1841, there were over half a million peasant farmers, with 1.75 million dependants. The principal beneficiary of this system was the English consumer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199520_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir199520-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Celtic grazing lands of ... Ireland had been used to pasture cows for centuries. The British colonised ... the Irish, transforming much of their countryside into an extended grazing land to raise cattle for a hungry consumer market at home ... The British taste for beef had a devastating impact on the impoverished and disenfranchised people of ... Ireland ... pushed off the best pasture land and forced to farm smaller plots of marginal land, the Irish turned to the potato, a crop that could be grown abundantly in less favorable soil. Eventually, cows took over much of Ireland, leaving the native population virtually dependent on the potato for survival.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERifkin199356\xe2\x80\x9357_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERifkin199356\xe2\x80\x9357-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The potato was also used extensively as a fodder crop for livestock immediately prior to the famine. Approximately 33% of production, amounting to 5,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a> (4,500,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">t</a>), was normally used in this way.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blight_in_Ireland">Blight in Ireland</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg/220px-Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg/330px-Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg/440px-Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2790" data-file-height="1582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Suggested_paths_of_migration_and_diversification_of_P._infestans_lineages_HERB-1_and_US-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Suggested paths of migration and diversification of <i>P. infestans</i> lineages HERB-1 and US-1</div></div></div>\n<p>Prior to the arrival in Ireland of the disease <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">Phytophthora infestans</a></i>, commonly known as blight, there were only two main potato plant diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200540_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200540-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> One was called "dry rot" or "taint", and the other was a virus known popularly as "curl".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200540_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200540-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinealy199432\xe2\x80\x9333_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinealy199432\xe2\x80\x9333-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <i>Phytophthora infestans</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Oomycete" title="Oomycete">oomycete</a> (a variety of parasitic, non-photosynthetic algae, and not a fungus).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaddock1992197\xe2\x80\x93222_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaddock1992197\xe2\x80\x93222-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1851, the Census of Ireland Commissioners recorded 24 <a href="/wiki/Harvest_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvest failure">failures</a> of the potato crop going back to 1728, of varying severity. General crop failures, through disease or frost, were recorded in 1739, 1740, 1770, 1800, and 1807. In 1821 and 1822, the potato crop failed in <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connacht" title="Connacht">Connaught</a>. In 1830 and 1831, <a href="/wiki/County_Mayo" title="County Mayo">Mayo</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">Donegal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/County_Galway" title="County Galway">Galway</a> suffered likewise. In 1832, 1833, 1834, and 1836, dry rot and curl caused serious losses, and in 1835 the potato failed in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>. Widespread failures throughout Ireland occurred in 1836, 1837, 1839, 1841, and 1844. According to Woodham-Smith, "the unreliability of the potato was an accepted fact in Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199138_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199138-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>How and when the blight <i>Phytophthora infestans</i> arrived in Europe is still uncertain; however, it almost certainly was not present prior to 1842, and probably arrived in 1844.<sup id="cite_ref-Bourke_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bourke-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The origin of the pathogen has been traced to <a href="/wiki/Toluca_Valley" title="Toluca Valley">Toluca Valley</a> of Mexico,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> whence it spread first within North America and then to Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Bourke_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bourke-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The 1845\xe2\x80\x9346 blight was caused by the HERB-1 strain of the blight.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg/290px-Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg.png" width="290" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg/435px-Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg/580px-Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1179" data-file-height="770" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Potatoes_Production_Great_Famine_en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potato production during the Great Famine.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Note: years 1844, 1845, 1846, and 1848 are extrapolated.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1844, Irish newspapers carried reports concerning a disease which for two years had attacked the potato crops in America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinealy199431_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinealy199431-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A likely source was the eastern United States, where in 1843 and 1844 blight largely destroyed the potato crops. Ships from <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> could have brought diseased potatoes to European ports.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200541_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200541-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> W. C. Paddock<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2017)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> posited that it was transported on potatoes being carried to feed passengers on <a href="/wiki/Clipper_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Clipper ship">clipper ships</a> sailing from America to Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaddock1992197\xe2\x80\x93222_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaddock1992197\xe2\x80\x93222-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Once introduced, it spread rapidly. By mid-August 1845, it had reached much of northern and central Europe; Belgium, The Netherlands, northern France, and southern England had all been stricken.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200542_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200542-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 16 August 1845, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gardeners%27_Chronicle" title="The Gardeners&#39; Chronicle">The Gardeners\' Chronicle</a> and Horticultural Gazette</i> reported "a blight of unusual character" on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a>. A week later, on 23 August, it reported that "A fearful malady has broken out among the potato crop&#160;... In Belgium the fields are said to be completely desolated. There is hardly a sound sample in <a href="/wiki/Covent_Garden" title="Covent Garden">Covent Garden market</a>&#160;... As for cure for this distemper, there is none."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199140_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199140-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> These reports were extensively covered in Irish newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinealy199432_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinealy199432-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> On 11 September, the <i><a href="/wiki/Freeman%27s_Journal" title="Freeman&#39;s Journal">Freeman\'s Journal</a></i> reported on "the appearance of what is called \'cholera\' in potatoes in Ireland, especially in the north".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> On 13 September,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;fn 1&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Gardeners\' Chronicle</i> announced: "We stop the Press with very great regret to announce that the potato Murrain has unequivocally declared itself in Ireland."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199140_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199140-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the British government remained optimistic over the next few weeks, as it received conflicting reports. Only when the crop was lifted in October did the scale of destruction become apparent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199140\xe2\x80\x9341,_43_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199140\xe2\x80\x9341,_43-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a> wrote to <a href="/wiki/Sir_James_Graham,_2nd_Baronet" title="Sir James Graham, 2nd Baronet">Sir James Graham</a> in mid-October that he found the reports "very alarming", but reminded him that there was, according to Woodham-Smith, "always a tendency to exaggeration in Irish news".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199141\xe2\x80\x9342_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199141\xe2\x80\x9342-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crop loss in 1845 has been estimated at anywhere from one third<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da20067_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da20067-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> to as high as one half of cultivated acreage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinealy199432_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinealy199432-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The Mansion House Committee in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, to which hundreds of letters were directed from all over Ireland, claimed on 19 November 1845 to have ascertained beyond the shadow of doubt that "considerably more than one-third of the entire of the potato crop ... has been already destroyed".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200542_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonnelly200542-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1846, three-quarters of the harvest was lost to blight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedyEllCrawfordClarkson199969_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedyEllCrawfordClarkson199969-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> By December, a third of a million destitute people were employed in public works.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss2002311_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss2002311-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Cormac_%C3%93_Gr%C3%A1da" title="Cormac \xc3\x93 Gr\xc3\xa1da">Cormac \xc3\x93 Gr\xc3\xa1da</a>, the first attack of potato blight caused considerable hardship in rural Ireland, from the autumn of 1846, when the first deaths from starvation were recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da20069_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da20069-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Seed potatoes were scarce in 1847. Little had been sown, so, despite average yields, hunger continued. 1848 yields would be only two-thirds of normal. Since over three million Irish people were totally dependent on potatoes for food, hunger and famine were inevitable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedyEllCrawfordClarkson199969_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedyEllCrawfordClarkson199969-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_in_Ireland">Reaction in Ireland</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Mitchel_(Young_Ireland).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/John_Mitchel_%28Young_Ireland%29.JPG/220px-John_Mitchel_%28Young_Ireland%29.JPG" width="220" height="354" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/John_Mitchel_%28Young_Ireland%29.JPG/330px-John_Mitchel_%28Young_Ireland%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/John_Mitchel_%28Young_Ireland%29.JPG/440px-John_Mitchel_%28Young_Ireland%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Mitchel_(Young_Ireland).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Mitchel" title="John Mitchel">John Mitchel</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Corporation_of_Dublin" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporation of Dublin">Corporation of Dublin</a> sent a memorial to the Queen, "praying her" to call Parliament together early (Parliament was at this time <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session" title="Legislative session">prorogued</a>), and to recommend the requisition of some public money for public works, especially railways in Ireland. The Town Council of <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a> met and made similar suggestions, but neither body asked for charity, according to <a href="/wiki/John_Mitchel" title="John Mitchel">John Mitchel</a>, one of the leading Repealers. "They demanded that, if Ireland was indeed an Integral part of the realm, the common exchequer of both islands should be used\xe2\x80\x94not to give alms, but to provide employment on public works of general utility ... if Yorkshire and Lancashire had sustained a like calamity in England, there is no doubt such measures as these would have been taken, promptly and liberally", Mitchel declared.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel200594\xe2\x80\x9396_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel200594\xe2\x80\x9396-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early November 1845, a deputation from the citizens of Dublin, including the <a href="/wiki/Augustus_FitzGerald,_3rd_Duke_of_Leinster" title="Augustus FitzGerald, 3rd Duke of Leinster">Duke of Leinster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Lawless,_2nd_Baron_Cloncurry" title="Valentine Lawless, 2nd Baron Cloncurry">Lord Cloncurry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor_of_Dublin" title="Lord Mayor of Dublin">Lord Mayor</a>, went to the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Ireland" title="Lord Lieutenant of Ireland">Lord Lieutenant of Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_%C3%A0_Court,_1st_Baron_Heytesbury" title="William \xc3\xa0 Court, 1st Baron Heytesbury">Lord Heytesbury</a>, to offer suggestions, such as opening the ports to foreign corn, stopping distillation from grain, prohibiting the export of foodstuffs, and providing employment through public works.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199148\xe2\x80\x9349_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199148\xe2\x80\x9349-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Lord Heytesbury urged them not to be alarmed, that they "were premature", that scientists were enquiring into all those matters,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;fn 2&#93;</a></sup> and that the Inspectors of Constabulary and Stipendiary Magistrates were charged with making constant reports from their districts; and there was no "immediate pressure on the market".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel200594\xe2\x80\x9396_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel200594\xe2\x80\x9396-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 8 December 1845, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a>, head of the <a href="/wiki/Repeal_Association" title="Repeal Association">Repeal Association</a>, proposed several remedies to the pending disaster. One of the first things he suggested was the introduction of "<a href="/wiki/Tenant-Right" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenant-Right">Tenant-Right</a>" as practised in Ulster, giving the landlord a fair rent for his land, but giving the tenant compensation for any money he might have laid out on the land in permanent improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel200596_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel200596-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> O\'Connell then pointed out the means used by the Belgian legislature during the same season: shutting their ports against the export of provisions, but opening them to imports. He suggested that, if Ireland had a domestic Parliament, the ports would be thrown open and the abundant crops raised in Ireland would be kept for the people of Ireland. O\'Connell maintained that only an <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Irish parliament</a> would provide for the people both food and employment, saying that a repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union</a> was a necessity and Ireland\'s only hope.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel200596_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel200596-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mitchel raised the issue of the "Potato Disease" in Ireland as early as 1844 in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nation_(Irish_newspaper)" title="The Nation (Irish newspaper)">The Nation</a></i>, noting how powerful an agent hunger had been in certain revolutions.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> On 14 February 1846, he put forward his views on "the wretched way in which the famine was being trifled with", and asked whether the Government still did not have any conception that there might be soon "millions of human beings in Ireland having nothing to eat".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Sullivan1945_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Sullivan1945-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg/220px-Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg/330px-Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg/440px-Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1291" data-file-height="1873" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Irish_potato_famine_Bridget_O%27Donnel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1849 depiction of Bridget O\'Donnell and her two children during the famine.</div></div></div>\n<p>On 28 February, writing on the <a href="/wiki/Coercion_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Coercion Bill">Coercion Bill</a> which was then going through the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, he noted that this was the only kind of legislation that was sure to meet with no obstruction in the British House of Commons. His view was that, however the government may differ about feeding the Irish people, "they agree most cordially in the policy of taxing, prosecuting and ruining them"<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (June 2015)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (as it happened, the bill was <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel#Corn_Laws_and_after" title="Robert Peel">subsequently defeated</a>, and Peel\'s government fell).\n</p><p>In an article on "English Rule" on 7 March, Mitchel wrote that the Irish People were "expecting famine day by day", and that they attributed it collectively not to "the rule of heaven as to the greedy and cruel policy of England". He continued in the same article that the people "believe that the season as they roll are but ministers of England\'s rapacity; that their starving children cannot sit down to their scanty meal but they see the harpy claw of England in their dish". He wrote that the people watched as their "food melting in rottenness off the face of the earth", all the while watching "heavy-laden ships, freighted with the yellow corn their own hands have sown and reaped, spreading all sail for England".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (June 2015)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Mitchel later wrote one of the first widely circulated tracts on the famine, <i>The Last Conquest of Ireland (Perhaps)</i>, in 1861. It established the widespread view that the treatment of the famine by the British was a deliberate murder of the Irish, and it contained the famous phrase: "The Almighty, indeed, sent the potato blight, but the English created the Famine."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2007312_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2007312-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Mitchel was charged with <a href="/wiki/Sedition" title="Sedition">sedition</a> because of his writings, but this charge was dropped and he was convicted by a packed jury under the newly enacted <a href="/wiki/Treason_Felony_Act_1848" title="Treason Felony Act 1848">Treason Felony Act</a> and sentenced to 14 years <a href="/wiki/Penal_transportation" title="Penal transportation">transportation</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2007323_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2007323-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gavan_Duffy" title="Charles Gavan Duffy">Charles Gavan Duffy</a>, <i>The Nation</i> insisted that the one remedy was that which the rest of Europe had adopted, which even the parliaments of <a href="/wiki/The_Pale" title="The Pale">the Pale</a> had adopted in periods of distress, which was to retain in the country the food raised by her people till the people were fed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy1888277\xe2\x80\x93278_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy1888277\xe2\x80\x93278-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Contemporaneously, as found in letters from the period and in particular later oral memory, the name for the event is in <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i lang="ga">An Drochshaol</i>, though with the earlier <a href="/wiki/Irish_language#Orthography" title="Irish language">spelling standard of the era</a>, which was <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_type" title="Gaelic type">Gaelic script</a>, it is found written as in <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i lang="ga">Dro\xc4\x8b-\xe1\xb9\xa0ao\xc4\xa1al</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The name, while loosely translated as "the hard-time", is in the modern era, always denoted with a capital letter to express its specific historic meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ireland at this time was, according to the Act of Union of 1801, an integral part of the British imperial homeland, "the richest empire on the globe", and was "the most fertile portion of that empire", in addition; Ireland was sheltered by both "<a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">Habeas corpus</a> and trial by jury".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel2005_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel2005-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (September 2018)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Despite this, Ireland\'s elected representatives seemed powerless to act on the country\'s behalf as Members of the British Parliament. Commenting on this at the time, Mitchel wrote: "That an island which is said to be an integral part of the richest empire on the globe ... should in five years lose two and a half millions of its people (more than one fourth) by hunger, and fever the consequence of hunger, and flight beyond sea to escape from hunger ..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel2005_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel2005-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (September 2018)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\nThe period of the potato blight in Ireland from 1845 to 1851 was full of political confrontation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir1995_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEP\xc3\xb3irt\xc3\xa9ir1995-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> A more radical <a href="/wiki/Young_Ireland" title="Young Ireland">Young Ireland</a> group seceded from the Repeal movement in July 1846, and attempted <a href="/wiki/Young_Irelander_Rebellion_of_1848" title="Young Irelander Rebellion of 1848">an armed rebellion in 1848</a>. It was unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991329\xe2\x80\x93360_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991329\xe2\x80\x93360-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg/220px-William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg/330px-William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg/440px-William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1095" data-file-height="1295" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Smith_O%27Brien.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Smith_O%27Brien" title="William Smith O&#39;Brien">William Smith O\'Brien</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1847, <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_O%27Brien" title="William Smith O&#39;Brien">William Smith O\'Brien</a>, leader of the Young Ireland party, became one of the founding members of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Confederation" title="Irish Confederation">Irish Confederation</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoheny1951_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoheny1951-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> to campaign for a Repeal of the Act of Union, and called for the export of grain to be stopped and the ports closed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel1869414_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel1869414-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The following year, he organised the resistance of landless farmers in <a href="/wiki/County_Tipperary" title="County Tipperary">County Tipperary</a> against the landowners and their agents.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_response">Government response</span></h2>\n<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/F._S._L._Lyons" title="F. S. L. Lyons">F. S. L. Lyons</a> characterised the initial response of the British government to the early, less severe phase of the famine as "prompt and relatively successful".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons197330_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons197330-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Confronted by widespread crop failure in November 1845, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a> purchased \xc2\xa3100,000 worth of maize and <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">cornmeal</a> secretly from America<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199154\xe2\x80\x9356_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199154\xe2\x80\x9356-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Barings_Bank" title="Barings Bank">Baring Brothers</a> initially acting as his agents. The government hoped that they would not "stifle private enterprise" and that their actions would not act as a disincentive to local relief efforts. Due to poor weather conditions, the first shipment did not arrive in Ireland until the beginning of February 1846.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinealy199447_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinealy199447-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The initial shipments were of unground dried kernels, but the few Irish mills in operation were not equipped for milling maize and a long and complicated milling process had to be adopted before the meal could be distributed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199164\xe2\x80\x9365_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199164\xe2\x80\x9365-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In addition, before the cornmeal could be consumed, it had to be "very much" cooked again, or eating it could result in severe bowel complaints.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinealy199447_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinealy199447-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Due to its yellow colour, and initial unpopularity, it became known as "Peel\'s brimstone".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199173_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199173-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 1845, Peel moved to repeal the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> on grain which kept the price of bread artificially high\xe2\x80\x94but the issue split his party and he had insufficient support from his own colleagues to push the measure through. He resigned the premiership in December, but the opposition was unable to form a government and he was re-appointed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199151\xe2\x80\x9352_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199151\xe2\x80\x9352-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In March, Peel set up a programme of public works in Ireland,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199178_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199178-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> but the famine situation worsened during 1846, and the repeal of the Corn Laws in that year did little to help the starving Irish; the measure split the Conservative Party, leading to the fall of Peel\'s ministry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake1967221\xe2\x80\x93241_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake1967221\xe2\x80\x93241-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> On 25 June, the <a href="/wiki/Second_reading" class="mw-redirect" title="Second reading">second reading</a> of the government\'s <a href="/wiki/Protection_of_Person_and_Property_Act_1881#Coercion_legislation_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Protection of Person and Property Act 1881">Irish Coercion Bill</a> was defeated by 73 votes in the House of Commons by a combination of <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radicals_(UK)" title="Radicals (UK)">Radicals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Repeal_Association" title="Repeal Association">Irish Repealers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionist</a> Conservatives. Peel was forced to resign as prime minister on 29 June, and the Whig leader, <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Lord John Russell</a>, assumed the seals of office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoheny195198_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoheny195198-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The measures undertaken by Peel\'s successor, Russell, proved comparatively inadequate as the crisis deepened. The new Whig administration, influenced by the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991410\xe2\x80\x93411_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991410\xe2\x80\x93411-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> believed that the market would provide the food needed, and they refused to intervene against food exports to England, then halted the previous government\'s food and relief works, leaving many hundreds of thousands of people without any work, money, or food.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss2002224,_311_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss2002224,_311-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Russell\'s ministry introduced a new programme of public works that by the end of December 1846 employed some half million Irish and proved impossible to administer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Doolough.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Doolough.jpg/220px-Doolough.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Doolough.jpg/330px-Doolough.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Doolough.jpg/440px-Doolough.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2399" data-file-height="1599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Doolough.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A memorial to the victims of the <a href="/wiki/Doolough_Tragedy" title="Doolough Tragedy">Doolough Tragedy</a> (30 March 1849). To continue receiving relief, hundreds were instructed to travel many miles in bad weather. A large number died on the journey.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sir_Charles_Trevelyan,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Charles Trevelyan, 1st Baronet">Charles Trevelyan</a>, who was in charge of the administration of government relief, limited the Government\'s food aid programme because of a firm belief in laissez-faire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199187,_106\xe2\x80\x93108_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199187,_106\xe2\x80\x93108-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> He thought that "the judgement of God sent the calamity to teach the Irish a lesson". The Public Works were "strictly ordered" to be unproductive\xe2\x80\x94that is, they would create no fund to repay their own expenses. Many hundreds of thousands of "feeble and starving men", according to Mitchel, were kept digging holes and breaking up roads, which was doing no service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel199616_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel199616-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (September 2018)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In January 1847, the government abandoned this policy, realising that it had failed, and turned to a mixture of "indoor" and "outdoor" direct relief; the former administered in <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouses</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Poor_Laws" title="Irish Poor Laws">Irish Poor Laws</a>, the latter through <a href="/wiki/Soup_kitchen" title="Soup kitchen">soup kitchens</a>. The costs of the Poor Law fell primarily on the local landlords, some of whom in turn attempted to reduce their liability by evicting their tenants,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> a practice that was facilitated by the "Cheap Ejectment Acts".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel199616_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel199616-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (September 2018)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In June 1847 the Poor Law Amendment Act was passed which embodied the principle, popular in Britain, that Irish property must support Irish poverty. The landed proprietors in Ireland were held in Britain to have created the conditions that led to the famine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERanelagh200060_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERanelagh200060-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991296\xe2\x80\x93297_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991296\xe2\x80\x93297-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> However, it was asserted that the British parliament since the Act of Union of 1800 was partly to blame.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERanelagh200060_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERanelagh200060-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> This point was raised in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">The Illustrated London News</a></i> on 13 February 1847: "There was no law it would not pass at their request, and no abuse it would not defend for them." On 24 March, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> reported that Britain had permitted in Ireland "a mass of poverty, disaffection, and degradation without a parallel in the world. It allowed proprietors to suck the very life-blood of that wretched race".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERanelagh200060_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERanelagh200060-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The "Gregory clause" of the Poor Law, named after <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Gregory" title="William Henry Gregory">William H. Gregory</a>, M.P.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;fn 3&#93;</a></sup>, prohibited anyone who held at least &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>4</sub></span> of an acre (0.1 ha) from receiving relief.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In practice, this meant that, if a farmer, having sold all his produce to pay rent and taxes, should be reduced, as many thousands of them were, to applying for public outdoor relief, he would not get it until he had first delivered up all his land to the landlord. Of this Law, Mitchel wrote that "it is the <a href="/wiki/Able-bodied" title="Able-bodied">able-bodied</a> idler only who is to be fed\xe2\x80\x94if he attempted to till but one <a href="/wiki/Rood_(unit)" title="Rood (unit)">rood</a> of ground, he dies". This simple method of ejectment was called "passing paupers through the workhouse"\xe2\x80\x94a man went in, a pauper came out.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchel199616_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchel199616-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> These factors combined to drive thousands of people off the land: 90,000 in 1849, and 104,000 in 1850.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons197330\xe2\x80\x9334-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1849 the <a href="/wiki/Encumbered_Estates%27_Court" title="Encumbered Estates&#39; Court">Encumbered Estates Act</a> allowed landlord estates to be auctioned off upon the petition of creditors. Estates with debts were then auctioned off at low prices. Wealthy British speculators purchased the lands and "took a harsh view" to the tenant farmers who continued renting. The rents were raised and tenants evicted to create large cattle grazing pastures. Between 1849 and 1854, some 50,000 families were evicted.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-historyplace_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historyplace-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (August 2018)">self-published source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_food_exports_during_Famine">Irish food exports during Famine</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan,_Co._Waterford,_Ireland,_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_(1846)_-_BL.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan%2C_Co._Waterford%2C_Ireland%2C_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_%281846%29_-_BL.jpg/220px-A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan%2C_Co._Waterford%2C_Ireland%2C_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_%281846%29_-_BL.jpg" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan%2C_Co._Waterford%2C_Ireland%2C_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_%281846%29_-_BL.jpg/330px-A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan%2C_Co._Waterford%2C_Ireland%2C_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_%281846%29_-_BL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan%2C_Co._Waterford%2C_Ireland%2C_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_%281846%29_-_BL.jpg/440px-A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan%2C_Co._Waterford%2C_Ireland%2C_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_%281846%29_-_BL.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1505" data-file-height="1369" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_food_riot_in_Dungarvan,_Co._Waterford,_Ireland,_during_the_famine_-_The_Pictorial_Times_(1846)_-_BL.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rioters in <a href="/wiki/Dungarvan" title="Dungarvan">Dungarvan</a> attempt to break into a <a href="/wiki/Bakery" title="Bakery">bakery</a>; the poor could not afford to buy what food was available. (<i>The Pictorial Times</i>, 1846).</div></div></div>\n<p>Records show that Irish lands exported food even during the worst years of the Famine. When Ireland had experienced a famine in 1782\xe2\x80\x9383, ports were closed to keep Irish-grown food in Ireland to feed the Irish. Local food prices promptly dropped. Merchants lobbied against the export ban, but government in the 1780s overrode their protests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrish_Famine_Curriculum_Committee199811_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrish_Famine_Curriculum_Committee199811-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> No such export ban happened in the 1840s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinealy1994354_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinealy1994354-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout the entire period of the Famine, Ireland was exporting enormous quantities of food. In the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/History_Ireland" title="History Ireland">History Ireland</a></i> (1997, issue 5, pp.&#160;32\xe2\x80\x9336), <a href="/wiki/Christine_Kinealy" title="Christine Kinealy">Christine Kinealy</a>, a Great Hunger scholar, lecturer, and <a href="/wiki/Drew_University" title="Drew University">Drew University</a> professor, relates her findings: Almost 4,000 vessels carried food from Ireland to the ports of Bristol, Glasgow, Liverpool, and London during 1847, when 400,000 Irish men, women, and children died of starvation and related diseases. She also writes that Irish exports of calves, livestock (except pigs), bacon, and ham actually increased during the Famine. This food was shipped from the most famine-stricken parts of Ireland: Ballina, Ballyshannon, Bantry, Dingle, Killala, Kilrush, Limerick, Sligo, Tralee, and Westport. A wide variety of commodities left Ireland during 1847, including peas, beans, onions, rabbits, salmon, oysters, herring, lard, honey, tongues, animal skins, rags, shoes, soap, glue, and seed. The most shocking export figures concern butter. Butter was shipped in firkins, each one holding 9 imperial gallons; 41 litres. In the first nine months of 1847, 56,557 firkins (509,010 imperial gallons; 2,314,000 litres) were exported from Ireland to Bristol, and 34,852 firkins (313,670 imperial gallons; 1,426,000 litres) were shipped to Liverpool, which correlates with 822,681 imperial gallons (3,739,980 litres) of butter exported to England from Ireland during nine months of the worst year of the Famine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrish_Famine_Curriculum_Committee199810_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrish_Famine_Curriculum_Committee199810-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The problem in Ireland was not lack of food, which was plentiful, but the price of it, which was beyond the reach of the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991165_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith1991165-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Woodham-Smith" title="Cecil Woodham-Smith">Cecil Woodham-Smith</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Hunger:_Ireland_1845%E2%80%931849" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Hunger: Ireland 1845\xe2\x80\x931849">The Great Hunger: Ireland 1845\xe2\x80\x931849</a></i> that no issue has provoked so much anger and embittered relations between England and Ireland "as the indisputable fact that huge quantities of food were exported from Ireland to England throughout the period when the people of Ireland were dying of starvation".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199175_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199175-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Ranelagh" title="John Ranelagh">John Ranelagh</a> writes that Ireland remained a net exporter of food throughout most of the five-year famine.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> However, both Woodham-Smith and Cormac \xc3\x93 Gr\xc3\xa1da write that, in addition to the maize imports, four times as much wheat was imported into Ireland at the height of the famine as exported.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199176_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodham-Smith199176-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da2000123_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da2000123-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Souperism" title="Souperism">Souperism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ridpath%27s_history_of_the_world_-_being_an_account_of_the_principal_events_in_the_career_of_the_human_race_from_the_beginnings_of_civilization_to_the_present_time,_comprising_the_development_of_social_(14749361956).jpg" class="image"><img alt=""
